тАШрдкреНрд░рдкреНрд░ рд╡рдГ’ рдЗрддреНрдпрд╖реНрдЯрд╛рджрд╢рд░реНрдЪрдВ рджрд╢рдордВ рд╕реВрдХреНрддрдВ рдкреНрд░рд┐рдпрдореЗрдзрд╕реНрдпрд╛рдЩреНрдЧрд┐рд░рд╕рд╕реНрдпрд╛рд░реНрд╖рдореН ред рджреНрд╡рд┐рддреАрдпрд╛ тАШрдирджрдВ рд╡рдГ’ рдЗрддреНрдпреЗрд╖рд╛ рдЪрддреБрдГрд╕рдкреНрддрдХреЛрд╖реНрдгрд┐рдХреН рдЪрддреБрд░реНрдереНрдпрд╛рджреНрдпрд╛рд╕реНрддрд┐рд╕реНрд░реЛ рдЧрд╛рдпрддреНрд░реНрдп рдПрдХрд╛рджрд╢реАрд╖реЛрдбрд╢реНрдпреМ рдкрдЩреНрдХреНрддреА рд╢рд┐рд╖реНрдЯрд╛ рджрд╢рд╛рдиреБрд╖реНрдЯреБрднрдГ редтАШрдЕрдкрд╛рджрд┐рдиреНрджреНрд░рдГ рдЗрддреНрдпрд░реНрдзрд░реНрдЪреЛ рд╡реИрд╢реНрд╡рджреЗрд╡реЛ тАШрд╡рд░реБрдг рдЗрджрд┐рд╣’ рдЗрддреНрдпрд╛рджреНрдпрд╛рд╕реНрддреНрд░рдпреЛрд╜рд░реНрдзрд░реНрдЪрд╛ рд╡рд░реБрдгрджреЗрд╡рддрд╛рдХрд╛рдГ рд╢рд┐рд╖реНрдЯрд╛ рдРрдиреНрджреНрд░реНрдпрдГ ред рддрдерд╛ рдЪрд╛рдиреБрдХреНрд░рд╛рдиреНрддрдВтАФтАШрдкреНрд░рдкреНрд░ рджреНрд╡реНрдпреВрдирд╛рдиреБрд╖реНрдЯреБрднрдВ рджреНрд╡рд┐рдмреГрд╣рддреНрдпрдиреНрддрдВ рджреНрд╡рд┐рддреАрдпреЛрд╖реНрдгрд┐рдХреН рдЪрддреБрд░реНрдереНрдпрд╛рджреНрдпрд╛рд╕реНрддрд┐рд╕реНрд░реЛ рдЧрд╛рдпрддреНрд░реНрдпрдГ рд╖реЛрд│рд╢реНрдпреЗрдХрд╛рджрд╢реНрдпреМ рдкрдЩреНрдХреНрддреА рдЕрдкрд╛рджреНрд╡реИрд╢реНрд╡рджреЗрд╡реЛрд╜рд░реНрдзрд░реНрдЪрд╕реНрддреНрд░рдпреЛ рд╡рд╛рд░реБрдгрд╛рдГ’ рдЗрддрд┐ ред рдЖрджреНрдпрд╕реНрддреГрдЪрдГ рд╖реЛрдбрд╢рд┐рд╢рд╕реНрддреНрд░ рдЖрдиреБрд╖реНрдЯреБрднрдГ ред рд╕реВрддреНрд░рд┐рддрдВ ‘рдЪ- тАШ рдкреНрд░рдкреНрд░ рд╡рд╕реНрддреНрд░рд┐рд╖реНрдЯреБрднрдорд┐рд╖рдорд░реНрдЪрдд рдкреНрд░рд╛рд░реНрдЪрдд ’ (рдЖрд╢реНрд╡. рд╢реНрд░реМ. рем. реи) рдЗрддрд┐ рее

Jamison Brereton

69 (678)
Indra (1тАУ10, 13тАУ18), All Gods (11ab), Varun╠гa
(11cdтАУ12)
Priyamedha ─Ас╣Еgirasa
18 verses: anuс╣гс╣нubh, except g─Бyatr─л 4тАУ6, paс╣Еkti 11, 16, br╠еhat─л 17тАУ18 [2 wrongly identi fied as uс╣гс╣Зih by Anukramaс╣З─л]
This often baffling hymn is metrically complex, as the above summary shows, and its metrical patterns do not always coincide with its intricate structure. Although many details remains obscure, the thematic outline of the hymn becomes clearer when that structure has been discerned. In our view, the hymn falls into two halves (vss. 1тАУ9, 10тАУ18), which are exactly parallel. Each consists of two tr╠еcas (1тАУ6, 10тАУ15), followed by a single verse celebrating the partnership between Indra and the poet (7, 16), and ending with two verses concerning the PriyamedhasтАЩ ritual offering to Indra (8тАУ9, 17тАУ18). The parallelism is especially clear in the final three verses of each half: both 7 and 16 contain the dual optative sacevahi тАЬmight we two become comradesтАЭ and concern a journey of a certain number of steps. In both 8тАУ9 and 17тАУ18 the Priyamedhas are explicitly mentioned. They are exhorted to ritual per formance in verses 8тАУ9, while in 17тАУ18, as is suitable in hymn-final verses, their per
formance is summarized. The parallelism between the opening tr╠еcas of both halves is much freer, though there are rough correspondences.
The relationship between the two tr╠еcas of the first half is noteworthy. The first tr╠еca (vss. 1тАУ3) is quite puzzling, with opaque phraseology and uncertain references; the second tr╠еca (vss. 4тАУ6) seems a double of the first, paraphrasing and explaining it, in a sort of global example of тАЬpoetic repairтАЭ (Jamison 2006). The second tr╠еca exhorts the poet to тАЬchant forthтАЭ to Indra (vs. 4), and the next two verses describe the preparation of soma for Indra, with the soma drops racing to the milk (con
ceptualized, as so often, as cows). With this in mind we can approach the first tr╠еca. In verse 1 the opening phrase тАЬforth, forthтАЭ recalls the similar phrase in verse 4 and allows the verb тАЬchantтАЭ to be supplied (see also vs. 8). In verse 1 the recipient of what is chanted, the poem, identified as тАЬtriс╣гс╣нubh refreshment,тАЭ is clearly Soma (тАЬthe dropтАЭ), not Indra as in verse 4, but the constructions are parallel. The poem is

then the subject of the second half of the verse, as it seeks to win something unspec ified. In our opinion the subject of verse 2 is this same poem, taking aim at Soma (the тАЬroaring bullтАЭ) among the cows, who represent the milk-mixture. In verse 3 the
soma mixture is prepared for Indra, and, it seems, for the rest of the gods. This matched pair of triplets is followed by the remarkable verse 7, in which the poet proposes a partnership between himself and Indra, a partnership to be sealed by the symbolic seven steps that in the later marriage ceremony make a marriage legal after the bride and groom have taken them, and that already in the R╠еgveda (X.8.4) are the symbolic act creating a contract or alliance. The phrase тАЬthe seven(th) step of the comradeтАЭ seems to refer to this ceremonial institution. In order to make this alliance the poet and Indra go to IndraтАЩs own home and drink the soma together, having made the journey over the surface of the sea of soma (in our view, though others consider this to be a reference to the sun). The relationship between poet and god is thus a surprisingly intimate one, remi niscent of that between the poet Vasiс╣гс╣нha and Varuс╣Зa in VII.86тАУ89, especially the verses describing their former comradeship with grammatical constructions and lexicon very similar to those here (VII.88.3тАУ5). It is also similar to nearby VIII.62.11, where the poet again proposes that he and Indra yoke themselves together. The first half of the hymn is then brought to a close by the aforemen tioned exhortation to the Priyamedhas to chant to Indra (vs. 8), with a tantaliz ing glimpse of what appear to be three musical instruments accompanying them (vs. 9).
The two tr╠еcas opening the second half of the hymn (vss. 10тАУ15) reverse the order of obscurity. The first (vss. 10тАУ12) is relatively straightforward and, at least to begin with, matches material in the first half: the speckled cows giving milk for the soma mixture in verse 10 remind us of the dappled cows and their milk in verse 3 (also 5тАУ6), and Indra and other gods partake of the soma in verse 11, though the pres
ence and role of Varuс╣Зa in verses 11тАУ12 are somewhat puzzling. However, it is the next tr╠еca (vss. 13тАУ15) that almost defeats the interpreter, especially verse 13. There is no agreement even on what god (or gods) the verse concerns, much less on what the actions in question are and whether the verse is a self-contained syntactic unit. We are inclined to take the verse as a suite of relative clauses referring to Indra, who is triumphantly proclaimed in verse 14. The second half of verse 14 alludes to the Emuс╣гa myth (also treated in VIII.77, etc.), in which, it seems, Indra as a boy slew a boar named Emuс╣гa by shooting through a mountain, and brought (or Viс╣гс╣Зu brought him) a rice porridge and some buffalo (which had been protected by the boar?) from the mountain. The next verse (15) most likely also concerns this myth, or it may refer to another of IndraтАЩs boyhood deeds. There are numerous difficulties in these three verses, and our translation is only provisional.
The following verse (16) brings us back to the present, with the poetтАЩs second proposition of fellowship to Indra, again in the course of a journey. And the final two verses (17тАУ18) summarize the PriyamedhasтАЩ ritual efforts and pronounces them even better than before. We cannot claim to have settled the many issues of this hymn, but the structure outlined above does give a context in which to evaluate the difficulties that remain.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬPresent your sacrificial food with a three-fold song of praise to Indu, gladdener of heroes; he will blessyou in your religious rites to the accompaniment of your sacrifice.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Purandhy─Б = baupraj├▒ay─Б; (RV 7.97.9):bahv─лr stuthi;

Indu = indra, he who rules (ind) or besprinkles (und) with rain;

Triс╣гс╣нubham = a song of praisegenerally

Jamison Brereton

Forth, (chant) forth your triс╣гс╣нubh refreshment to the drop that
invigorates heroes.
With your visionary thought and with plenitude it is seeking to win, in order to gain wisdom.

Jamison Brereton Notes

It is necessary to supply a verb for ab; Geldner тАЬTragetтАЭ but I supply тАЬchantтАЭ on the basis of the beginning of the second tс╣Ыca, 4ab abh├н pr├б тАж arca, and the insistent repetition in vs. 8: ├бrcata pr─Б╠Бrcata, тАж ├бrcata# / ├бrcantu тАж arcata#. P─Бda a is essentially identical to VIII.7.1a pr├б y├бd vas triс╣гс╣н├║bham ├нс╣гam, where the verb governing the acc. is ├бkс╣гarat тАШhas let flowтАЩ (p─Бda b), which is not possible here. The тАЬTriс╣гс╣нubh refreshmentтАЭ is obviously the praise hymn; interestingly neither this passage nor VIII.7 is in Triс╣гс╣нubh meter.

The second hemistich is somewhat obscure, in part because, as Geldner points out, it is not clear who the subject is. Perhaps the тАЬTriс╣гс╣нubh refreshmentтАЭ (that is, the praise), or the drop (that is, the soma) to which it is chanted, or the poet. (I favor the praise hymn.) The pair dh─л╠Б- and p├║raс╣Гdh─л- appears elsewhere together (VIII.92.15, X.65.13-14). Geldner takes them here as names of the goddesses of religious poetry and of the dakс╣гin─Б respectively. In VIII.92.15 I take them as qualities that Indra confers on us, but here at least the dh─л╠Б- is probably that of the poet(s). Perhaps they are a reciprocal, rather than parallel, pair: we receive plenty in exchange for insight (as in V.41.6). The interpretation is further complicated by the fact that ─Б╠Бviv─Бsaeverywhere else takes an accusative, often in conjunction with an instr. of means: тАЬseek to attract X with YтАЭ (e.g., VIII.15.1 ├нndraс╣Г g─лrbh├нs taviс╣г├бm ─Б╠Бviv─Бsata), but there is no accusative immediately available. (Though vaс╕е could technically be an accusative, that interpretation seems unlikely here.) However, this is of course an Indra hymn, and in thinking about this verse again, I have concluded that Indra should be supplied here as the default object, and therefore for the published translation I would substitute тАЬWith your visionary thought and with plenitude it [=hymn] is seeking to entice (Indra) here, in order to gain wisdom.тАЭ Cf. VIII.3.18 im├й h├н te k─Бr├бvo v─Бva┼Ы├║r dhiy─Б╠Б, v├нpr─Бso medh├бs─Бtaye тАЬFor these bards, inspired poets, have bellowed for you [=Indra] for the winning of wisdom with their insight,тАЭ with both dhiy─Б╠Бand medh├бs─Бtaye in a similar context, where poets seek Indra.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ(Invoke) for yourselves the author of the dawns, (I invoke) for you the roarer of the rivers; (I invoke) foryou the lord of the inviolable ones; (O sacrificer), you desire kine.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Author of the dawns: anda = utp─Бdaka; Indrais called he author of the dawns as being identified with the sun, as one of the twelve ─Аdityas; inviolable ones:aghny─Бn─Бm = cows

Jamison Brereton

You take aim at the roaring bull among your moist [=willing] females and at the roaring bull among the females who keep hanging back,
at the lord of your prized milk-cows.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Another verse with unclear reference: neither the 2nd singular subject of iс╣гudhyasi nor the bull and cows are clearly identified, and it is complicated by the fact that there are also two occurrences of 2nd plural vaс╕е in the verse. I take the subject to be the same as that of ─Б╠Бviv─Бsati in 1d, namely the praise hymn, with the common switch of persons. That the praise hymn is in reality inanimate is not in RVic discourse an obstacle to addressing it. The 2nd pl. vaс╕е is then the same group of poets / ritual officiants as in 1a and 1c. As for the bull (nad├б-, a and b) and the p├бti- (c), I agree with Geldner (n. 2) that it can be either Indra or soma. In the latter case, the referent is then the same as the ├нndu- тАШdropтАЩ that was the target of the hymn in 1ab; in the latter, the same as the unexpressed object of ─Б╠Бviv─Бsati in 1cd. The cows are easier to account for if soma is the referent: they would then be the mixing milk as so often. If the bull is Indra, the cows may be the cows he brings us as largesse or praise hymns conceived of as cows (many words for hymn are feminine), and the sexual tinge of ab would be a nod to IndraтАЩs hypersexuality. Probably both referents are meant.

On iс╣гudhyasi see comm. ad I.128.6 and my forthcoming article тАЬVedic iс╣гudhy├б- and Oldenberg Avestan i┼бud-, i┼б┼лidiia-: The Aim of Praise.тАЭ Since I analyze

*iс╣гudh- as a root noun cmpd тАШaim an arrowтАЩ, a metaphor for aiming praise towards a divine target, the nad├бm тАж p├бtim must refer to the god receiving praise. Indra or Soma are both possible, of course.

Thou wishest for thy kine a bull, for those who long for his approach,
For those who turn away from him, lord of thy cows whom none may kill.

Auf euren Stier der quellenden, den Stier der sich vermischenden, Auf euren Herrn der milchenden, der K├╝he zielst du opfernd hin.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬThese white kine, giving milk wells, mix the Soma for him at the three oblations, rising (in consequece tothe brilliant home of the sun, the birthplace of the gods).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

When the bright fertilizing rivers (or the white cows,pravс╣Ыddha) flow with full waters (or with distended udders), then take…; (RV 6.48.11): dhenum sс╣Ыjadhvamanapasphur─Бm = release the cow unobstructed, anapab─Бdhan─лy─Бm ; (RV 4.42.10): tam dhenum dhattamanapasphurant─лm, grant us that cow (riches) uninured, anavahiс╣Гsit─Бm;

Alternaive: anapasphurant─лm = notgoing to another, i.e., not running away;

Anapauraс╕е = not struggling against being milked;

Apasphur─Бm =bursting forth, i.e. when the white cows come without starting away, ten take the gushing Soma for Indra todrink

Jamison Brereton

These dappled cows, which give the sweetening milk, prepare the soma for him [=Indra].
At its birth the clans of the gods are in the three luminous realms of heaven.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The first two p─Бdas seem rather briskly to clear up the uncertainties of the previous verse, or give at least one reading thereof: soma, the cows that give the milk to mix with soma, and Indra (though not by name) are all present, and their relationships are clear.

The connection of the last half of the verse to what precedes is not clear, as Geldner also points out. The birth is likely to be that of soma, but why are we now concerned with тАЬthe clans of the godsтАЭ? I tentatively assume that the gods are awaiting their invitation to the soma-drinking in their usual abodes. Somewhat later in the hymn (11b) all the gods partake of the soma.

There is number discrepancy between pl. triс╣г├║ and sg. rocan├й, as also in the identical p─Бda in I.105.5b. On the basis of expressions like I.102.8 tr─л╠Бс╣Зi rocan─Б╠Б, V.69.1 tr─л╠Бrocan─Б╠Б, I.149.4 tr─л╠Бrocan─Б╠Бni the two words must be construed together and a truncation of rocan├йс╣гu must be assumed. I donтАЩt quite know why, but Bloomfield (Rig-Veda Repetitions ad I.105.5) points out that rocan├й div├бс╕е is a frequent cadence, and it therefore may have imposed itself formulaically in this phrase. Also the alternation between tr─л╠Б and tr─л╠Бс╣Зi and rocan─Б╠Бand rocan─Б╠Бni in the formula may have made an alternative rocan├й to rocan├йс╣гu seem acceptable. In fact loc. pl. rocan├йс╣гu is only attested once.

Griffith

The dappled kine who stream with milk prepare his draught of Soma juice:
Clans in the birth-place of the Gods, in the three luminous realms of heaven.

Geldner

Diese scheckigen K├╝he, die die Zutat zum Soma milchen, mischen seinen Soma. Bei seiner Geburt sind der G├╢tter Clane in den drei Lichtr├дumen des Himmels.

Grassmann

Die K├╝he str├╢mend s├╝sse Milch, erw├дrmen seinen Opfertrank; In den drei Himmeln ist vereint der G├╢tter Schar, wenn er entspringt.

English translation:

тАЬWorship with your praise, as he himself knows– that lord of kine, Indra, the son of Satya, the protectorof the good.тАЭ

Satya = truth, yaj├▒a

Jamison Brereton

Chant forth with a song to the lord of cows, to Indra, in the way that is known,
to the son of the real, the master of settlements.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Although this verse belongs to a different tс╣Ыca, it seems to provide an alternative resolution to the puzzle of vs. 2, somewhat different from that given in 3ab. Here Indra is definitively identified as a g├│pati- тАШlord of cowsтАЩ, paraphrasing 2cd p├бtim тАж ├бghny─Бn─Бс╣Г dhen┼лn─Б╠Бm.

Geldner takes y├бth─Б vid├й as тАЬwie er bekannt ist,тАЭ but this tag phrase regularly refers to the traditional way of producing poetry or performing ritual. See, e.g., I.132.2, VIII.13.14, IX.86.32.

saty├бsya s├бtpatim is probably a pseudo-pun, given that by many accounts (including ours) s├бt-pati- derives from s├бts-pati-, with the first member a reduction of s├бdas- тАШseat, settlementтАЩ. See EWA s.v. If s├бt- belongs to тИЪas (as some think), it is a real pun.

The Son of Truth, Lord of the brave.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬLet the brilliant bay (horses) drop him down on the cut grass, where we will hymn his praise.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

The tawny (soma-drops) have been let loose at the ruddy (cows) upon the ritual grass,
where we will bellow out together.

Griffith

Hither his Bay Steeds have been sent, red Steeds are on the sacred grass,,
Where we in concert sing our songs.

Die Falben sind auf die roten K├╝he auf dem Barhis losgelassen, wobei wir in das Gebr├╝ll mit einstimmen.

Grassmann

Die goldigen ergossen sich, die Flammenrosse auf die Streu, Wo wir lobsingen allesammt.

English translation:

тАЬThe cows have milked the intoxicating draught for Indra, the thunderer, when he finds it near him.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

The cows have milked out the milk-mixture for Indra, the honey for the mace-bearer,
since he found them in the remote place.

Jamison Brereton Notes

P─Бda c is presumably a reference to the Vala cave, as Geldner points out.

Griffith

For Indra Thunder-armed the kine have yielded mingled milk and meath,
What time he found them in the vault.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬWhen Indra, and I ascend to our home, the world of the sun, then, having drunk the sweet (Soma), letus be united in the twenty-first sphere of the (universal) friend.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

As we two, Indra and (I), go up to his home along the surface of the coppery (soma),
having drunk of the honey three times, might we two become comrades at the seven(th) step of the comrade.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Another elliptical 1st dual construction; for disc. see VIII.62.11.

Geldner and Hoffmann, inter alia, take bradhn├бsya viс╣гс╣н├бpam to refer to the height or top of the sun. The phrase occurs also in IX.113.10. bradhn├б- in VIII.4.13-14 seems to refer to soma. Since viс╣гс╣н├бp- several times occurs with samudr├бsya (VIII.34.13, 97.5=IX.12.6, IX.107.14), something liquid makes sense, rather than wandering around on top of the sun. Furthermore, at least in IX.12.6 (and probably IX.107.14) the тАЬseaтАЭ in this expression is clearly soma. I also think that it works better as acc. of extent, rather than as goal, since the goal is the grh├бm ╠е .

For the ceremonial aspects of the second hemistich see published introduction. Note that s├бkhi- is an integral part of the тАЬseventh stepтАЭ mantra at the wedding ceremony: sakh─Б saptapad─л bhava тАЬBecome a comrade of the seventh stepтАЭ (┼ЪGS I.14.6, etc.; see Jamison 1996: 121). Contra Hoffmann (1967: 255) I separate tr├нс╕е from sapt├б, and consider the former to refer to drinking soma at the three pressings (so also Geldner). I also take the cardinal sapt├б as if it were the ordinal sapt├бtha-. For the corresponding verse, see 16 below.

Griffith

When I and Indra mount on high up to the Bright One’s place and home,
We, having drunk of meath, will reach his seat whose Friends are three times seven.

Geldner

Wenn ich und Indra, wir beide, nach Hause zur H├╢he der Sonne hinaufsteigen, so wollen wir vom Met trinkend dreimal in den sieben Schritten des Freundes zusammengehen.

Grassmann

Wenn zu des lichten Rosses Haus und St├дtte Indra geht mit mir, So bleiben nach des Soma Trunk, drei Wochen wir an Freundes Ort.

English translation:

тАЬThe drum utters its sound, the leathern guard twangs, the tawny bowstring leaps to and fro; let thehymn be raised to Indra.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

The Gargara(-instrument) will gurgle downward, the Godh─Б(-v─лс╣З─Б) will keep resounding all around,
and the Piс╣Еg─Б(-string) will keep quivering all around. The sacred
formulation is offered up to Indra.

Jamison Brereton Notes

It is likely that all three of these obscure words, g├бrgara-, godh─Б╠Б-, and p├нс╣Еg─Б-, are musical instruments, but further specification is difficult. VIII.69.11-12: I do not understand why Varuс╣Зa is mentioned specially. Perhaps because of the waters of 11d and the rivers of 12b?

Shrill be, the music of the string. To Indra. is the hymn up-raised.

English translation:

тАЬWhen the bright fertilizing rivers flow with diminished waters, then take the overflowing Soma for Indrato drink.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

When the speckled (cows), who yield good milk and never kick, fly here, take hold of the kicking soma for Indra to drink.

Griffith

When bither speed the dappled cows, unflinching, easy to be milked,
Seize quickly, as it bursts away, the Soma juice for Indra’s drink.

Geldner

Wenn die bunten, leicht zu melkenden, sich nicht entziehenden K├╝he herbeieilen, so haltet fest den sich entziehenden Soma f├╝r Indra zum Trinken!

Grassmann

Wenn kommt der bunten K├╝he Schar, die ohne Str├дuben Milch ergiesst, So fasst den Soma, der sich str├дubt, Zum Trunk dem Indra kr├дftig an.

English translation:

тАЬIndra drank (the Soma), Agni drank it, the Vi┼Ыve Dev─Бs were gladdened; let Varuс╣Зa fix his dwellinghere, the waters have praised him as cows (low) meeting their calves.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

The waters: apaс╕е = hymns, ─Бpana┼Ы─лlaс╕е

Jamison Brereton

Indra has drunk; Agni has drunk. All the gods have become
exhilarated.
Varuс╣Зa will settle here, too. The waters have bellowed out to him, as (cows) that share their young do to their calf.

Griffith

Indra hath drunk, Agni hath drunk. all Deities have drunk their fill.
Here Varuna shall have his home, to whom the floods have sung aloud as motherkine unto their calves.

Geldner

рд╣реЗ рд╡рд░реБрдг рдЬрд▓рд╛рднрд┐рдорд╛рдирд┐рдиреН рджреЗрд╡ рддреНрд╡рдВ рд╕реБрджреЗрд╡реЛрд╜рд╕рд┐ рдпрд╕реНрдп рд╕реБрджреЗрд╡рд╕реНрдп рддреЗ рддрд╡ рдХрд╛рдХреБрджрдВ рддрд╛рд▓реБ рд╕рдореБрджреНрд░рд╛рдЦреНрдпрдВ рд╕рдкреНрдд рд╕рд┐рдиреНрдзрд╡рдГ рдЧрдЩреНрдЧрд╛рджреНрдпрд╛рдГ рд╕рдкреНрдд рдирджреНрдпрдГ рдЕрдиреБрдХреНрд╖рд░рдиреНрддрд┐ рдЬрд┐рд╣реНрд╡рд╛рдпрд╛рдВ рд╕рд░реНрд╡рджрд╛ рд╕реНрд░рд╡рдиреНрддрд┐ рее

рдорд╛рдирд╕рддрд░рдЩреНрдЧрд┐рдгреАрдХреГрддреН

You are the good god o varuNa,
whose are the seven rivers
they follow through your throat
like through a pipe for good flow.

рдорд╛рдирд╕рддрд░рдЩреНрдЧрд┐рдгреАрдХреГрддреН - рдЯрд┐рдкреНрдкрдиреА

The peculiar simile suggests that the Indo-Aryans were familiar with pipes to convey water. Of course drain pipes are well known from Harappan sites. Such technology could have been more widely know and also used for supplying water from a reservoir.

In fact my thinking was that this might be a structure similar to a (Iranian) qanat – or something like that. The priyamedha-s were proximal to the branches that became Iranic.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬYou are a glorious god, Varuс╣Зa, across whose palate the seven rivers keep pouring as a fair-flowing(stream) into an abyss.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Across whose palate: s┼лrmyam suс╣гir─Бm iva: (Y─Бska Nirukta 5.27); grammariansadvocate the study of grammar; seven rivers are the seven declensional affixes; across whose palate the sevenrivers keep flowing a (fire penetrates and purifies) a beautiful perforated metal image); seven rivers are Gangaetc. and Varuс╣Зa’s palate is the ocean

Jamison Brereton

You are well-provided with gods, Varuс╣ЗaтАФyou through whose gullet the seven rivers
stream, as through a tube that provides easy flow.

Jamison Brereton Notes

I take sudev├б- as a bahuvr─лhi, as su-compounds with this metrical shape and accentuation generally are (contra GeldnerтАЩs тАЬein guter GottтАЭ). The gods mentioned in 11b may be part of Varuс╣ЗaтАЩs retinue.

Griffith

Thou, Varuna, to whom belong Seven Rivers, art a glorious God.
The waters flow into thy throat as ’twere a pipe with ample mouth.

Geldner

Du bist ein guter Gott, o Varuna, in dessen Schlund die sieben Str├╢me flie├Яen wie in eine hohle R├╢hre.

Grassmann

Ein rechter Gott bist du, o Varuna, in dessen Schlund die sieben Str├╢me hineinrinnen, wie in eine Wasserr├╢the, die gutes Gef├дlle hat.

English translation:

тАЬHe ho directs towards the worshiper his well-yoked prancing steeds– he, (Indra), the swift bearer ofblessing, (produces) rain– he, who being comparable only to himself is delivered (from all his enemies).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Comparable only to himself: upam─Б = upam─Бna bh┼лta; or, in close proximity

Jamison Brereton

He who made the paired, well-yoked horses leap for the pious man, (who is) their swooping leaderтАФand just this is the marvelтАФwho was set loose as the very measure (of heaven)тАФ

Jamison Brereton Notes

Interpretations of this verse vary, and cd is characterized as тАЬdunkelтАЭ by both Geldner and Oldenberg I take the verse as a series of relative clauses referring to (unnamed) Indra, leading up to the proclamation of Indra in 14ab. The relative clauses are interrupted by an exclamatory t├бd ├нd v├бpuс╕е тАЬjust this is the marvel!тАЭ in c.

Geldner takes takv├бс╕е as a PN (rejected by Mayr. PN; see also EWA s.v. TAK), but I see no reason to separate the stem from t├бkvan-, takva-v─л╠Б-, and takvav─л╠Бya-, all of which seem to refer to the movements of birds of prey.

I take upam─Б╠Б(so Pp., or upam─Б╠Бс╕е) in the same way as in I.31.15, viz., as a root noun compound тАШmeasureтАЩ. See disc. there.

Griffith

He who hath made the fleet steeds spring, well-harnessed, to the worshipper,
He, the swift Guide, is that fair form that loosed the horses near at hand.

Geldner

Der die wohlgeschirrten Gespanne f├╝r den Opfernden in Galopp setzte. Takva ist der Leiter - das ist das Wunder - der zun├дchst losgebunden wurde.

Grassmann

Welcher die sch├╢ngeschirrten Renner hinspringen liess zu dem Verehrer, der schnelle Lenker, der ganz in der N├дhe die herrliche Gestalt ausspannte.

English translation:

тАЬ┼Ъakra verily overpowers, Indra overpowers all his enemies; he, worthy of love, abiding beyond, cleavesthe cloud smitten by his thunder-voice.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Cloud smitten: odanam pacyam─Бnam = rice when cooked;

Odana = acloud (Y─Бska Naigh. 1.10);

Pacyam─Бna = t─Бс╕Нyam─Бna

Jamison Brereton

Just he is solemnly proclaimed as the able one, as Indra, beyond all hatreds.
The lad ripped (out) the rice porridge that was cooking far away on the mountain.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Geldner takes ohate as reflexive, but I prefer a passive interpretation here.

The second half of the verse segues into the Emuс╣гa myth prominent in this part of VIII. For disc. see published introduction.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ(Indra), like a young boy, has mounted his splendid chariot; he makes ready for his father and motherthe great deer-like many-functioned cloud.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

He makes ready: pac = to cook, to mature, vс╣Ыс╣гс╣нyabhimukham karoti,Indra makes the cloud ready for raining;

Mс╣Ыga = deer-like, wandering hither and thither like a deer, to be soughtby all; He roasts (with his thunderbolt) the wild mighty buffalo (the cloud) for his father and mother

Jamison Brereton

Like a teeny-tiny wee little boy, he mounted his new chariot.
He cooked the wild buffalo of prodigious will for his father and
mother.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Note the extreme diminutivization in arbhak├│ n├б kum─Бrak├бс╕е, signalled by the two -k├б-suffixed forms. Presumably the point, in this account of Boyhood Deeds, is to emphasize how small and weak he was to accomplish such deeds.

Both Geldner and Oldenberg are troubled by vibhukr├бtu- as a qualifier of the buffalo. Oldenberg goes so far as to suggest it should be nominative, but was attracted into the accusative by the preceding accusatives (which, note, are not even adjacent or in the same p─Бda). This seems unlikely (and unworthy of the usually punctilious Oldenberg). Geldner also manoeuvres the adjective into referring not to the kr├бtu- of the buffalo, but rather to that of the father and mother: тАЬEr kochte das B├╝ffeltier f├╝r Vater und Mutter, das ihrem Verlangen gen├╝gte.тАЭ I do not understand how he is interpreting the vibhu-, nor do I see any difficulty with assigning the quality named in the compound to the buffalo: that the buffalo is formidable makes the feat of a tiny boy cooking it (and presumably first catching it) all the greater.

Griffith

He, yet a boy exceeding small, mounted his newly-fashioned car.
He for his Mother and his Sire cooked the wild mighty buffalo.

Geldner

Wie ein kleines Kn├дbchen bestieg er den neuen Wagen. Er kochte das B├╝ffeltier f├╝r Vater und Mutter, das ihrem Verlangen gen├╝gte.

Grassmann

Als kleiner, zarter Knabe schon bestieg den neuen Wagen er; Dem Vater und der Mutter briet den kraftbegabten B├╝ffel er.

рд╣реЗ рд╕реБрд╢рд┐рдкреНрд░ рд╕реБрд╣рдиреЛ рд╣реЗ рджрдВрдкрддреЗ рдЧреГрд╣рд╕реНрд╡рд╛рдорд┐рдиреН ред рдЕрддреНрд░ рдЧреГрд╣реЛ рд░рдердГред рддрд╕реНрдп рд╕реНрд╡рд╛рдорд┐рдиреН ред рддреБ рддреНрд╡рдВ рддрд╛рд╡рддреН рд░рдердореН рдЖ рддрд┐рд╖реНрдаред рд╣рд╡рд┐рдГрд╕реНрд╡реАрдХрд░рдгрд╛рдиреНрддрд░рдВ рдкрд╢реНрдЪрд╛рджрд╣рдорд╛рд░реЛрд╣рд╛рдореАрддрд┐ рднрд╛рд╡рдГ ред рдХреАрджреГрд╢рдореН ред рд╣рд┐рд░рдгреНрдпрдпрдВ рд╣рд┐рд░рдгреНрдордпрдореНред рдЕрдз рддрд╡рд╛рд░реЛрд╣рдгрд╛рдирдиреНрддрд░рдорд╣рдордкреНрдпрд╛рд░реБрд╣реНрдпреЛрднреМ рд╕рдЪреЗрд╡рд╣рд┐ рд╕рдВрдЧрдЪреНрдЫреЗрд╡рд╣рд┐ ред рд╕рдВрдЧрддреМ рднрд╡реЗрд╡ред рдкреБрдирдГ рдХреАрджреГрд╢рдореН ред рджреНрдпреБрдХреНрд╖рдВ рджреАрдкреНрддрдВ рд░рдердВ рд╕рд╣рд╕реНрд░рдкрд╛рджрдВ рдмрд╣реБрдкрд╛рджрдореН рдЕрд░реБрд╖рдореН рдЖрд░реЛрдЪрдорд╛рдирдВ рд╕реНрд╡рд╕реНрддрд┐рдЧрд╛рдВ рдХреБрд╢рд▓рдЧрдордирдореН рдЕрдиреЗрд╣рд╕рдореН рдЕрдкрд╛рдкрдореН рее

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬHandsome-jawed (Indra), householder, mount your golden chariot; then let us meet mounted togetheron that bright thousand-footed brilliant auspiciously moving sinless (car).тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

You fair-lipped houselord, mount your golden chariot.
Then we two might become comrades along the heavenly, ruddy (path) with a thousand steps,
faultless and leading to well-being.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The first hemistich seems addressed to the present-day Indra, recalling his boyhood deeds recounted in 15ab.

The second half matches vs. 7, with the same proposition that Indra and I (=poet, in my opinion; Geldner rather Viс╣гс╣Зu) should become comrades (both times with sacevahi). The referent of the multiple accusatives in cde is disputed. Oldenberg suggests the sun, in part because sah├бsrap─Бd- is used of the sun already in AV VII.41.2. Geldner also considers the sun the referent of the accusatives in cd, тАЬden himmlischen, tausendstrahligen, r├╢tlichen (S┼лrya),тАЭ but separates those in e into a separate constituent (as far as I can tell), тАЬden fehlerlosen, zum Heile f├╝hrenden (Weg) folgen,тАЭ because p─Бda e is found in VI.51.16ab ├бpi p├бnth─Бm aganmahi, svastig─Б╠Бm aneh├бsam qualifying тАШpathтАЩ. I prefer to read all the accusatives together and take the path (of VI.51.16) as the referent. This also involves interpreting sah├бsrap─Бdam as тАШhaving a thousand stepsтАЩ, not тАШтАж a thousand feetтАЩ. This recalls vs. 7 тАЬтАж become comrades at the seven(th) stepтАж,тАЭ and I would suggest that the тАЬthousand stepтАЭ version plays off the previous one, indicating that our comradeship will last even longer. The path itself may be the soma (see disc. of 7) that facilitated the establishment of our comradeship; see 7c.

Griffith

Lord of the home, fair-helmeted, ascend thy chariot wrought of gold.
We will attend the Heavenly One, the thousand-footed, red of hue, matchless, who blesses where he goes.

Geldner

Besteige doch, du sch├╢nlippiger Hausherr, den goldenen Wagen, dann wollen wir beide dem himmlischen, tausendstrahligen, r├╢tlichen Surya den fehlerlosen, zum Heile f├╝hrenden Weg folgen.

Grassmann

Besteige, o Hausherr [Indra], der du sch├╢nen Helm tr├дgst, den goldenen Wagen, dann wollen wir beide hinstreben zu dem himmlischen, tausendf├╝ssigen, feuerrothen, zum Heile f├╝hrenden, unvergleichlichen [Agni].

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ(The priests), presenting praise, thus worship that self-resplendent (Indra); they obtain his well-storedwealth, when (his horsesk) bring him on his way for the offering.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

His horses: or, their praises

Jamison Brereton

Bringing homage, they reverently approach him, their sovereign king, just so.
Just this is his well-set goal when they turn him here to come and
to give. с│У

Note the responsion of 17b тАж ─Бsate# and 18d тАж ─Б┼Ыata#.

Griffith

With reverence they come hitherward to him as to. a Sovran lord,
That they may bring him near for this man’s good success, to prosper and bestow his gifts.

Geldner

Ihn erwarten sie hier unter Verbeugungen, den Selbstherrscher. Sein Zweck ist sogar voll erreicht, wenn sie ihn zum Kommen, zum Schenken bewegen.

Grassmann

Denn ihn vor allem, den selbststrahlenden [Indra] verehren die Anbetungsvollen; all sein Werk ist wol eingerichtet, wenn sie ihn herwenden, dass er schenke.
